## Chapter: Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will delve into the complex and often controversial topic of the ethics of intervention by great powers. As we have seen throughout history, powerful nations have often used their military might to intervene in the affairs of other countries, whether for strategic, political, or humanitarian reasons. This has sparked debates and discussions about the morality and legality of such actions, and the potential consequences they may have on both the intervening nation and the target nation.



We will explore the various ethical frameworks and principles that have been used to justify or condemn military intervention by great powers. This includes the concept of just war, which outlines the conditions under which a war can be considered morally justifiable. We will also examine the role of international law and organizations in regulating and overseeing military intervention, and the challenges and limitations they face in doing so.



Furthermore, we will analyze case studies of past interventions by great powers, both successful and unsuccessful, to understand the impact and implications of such actions. This will provide us with a deeper understanding of the complexities and nuances involved in the decision to intervene, and the potential consequences for all parties involved.



Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive and objective study of the ethics of intervention by great powers, shedding light on the various perspectives and considerations that must be taken into account when evaluating such actions. It is our hope that this will contribute to a more informed and nuanced understanding of this important and often contentious issue.





# Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



## Chapter 8: The Ethics of Intervention



### Section 8.1: Defining Ethical Intervention



In this section, we will explore the concept of ethical intervention and its various interpretations. Ethical intervention refers to the use of military force by a powerful nation to intervene in the affairs of another nation, with the intention of promoting a certain set of values or principles. This can include humanitarian intervention, where the goal is to protect the rights and well-being of individuals within the target nation, or strategic intervention, where the goal is to advance the interests of the intervening nation.



The concept of ethical intervention has a long history, with various philosophers and political thinkers offering their perspectives on its justification and limitations. One of the earliest proponents of ethical intervention was the Victorian liberal John Stuart Mill, who argued that there are cases where it is morally justifiable for a powerful nation to intervene in the affairs of a weaker nation. However, Mill's justification for intervention was rooted in overt imperialism, as he believed that "barbarous" nations could benefit from the intervention of a more civilized nation.



This idea of a "civilizing mission" has been heavily criticized in modern discourse, as it perpetuates a colonial mindset and ignores the agency and sovereignty of the target nation. However, Mill's argument highlights the importance of considering the intentions and motivations behind ethical intervention, as they can greatly impact the outcome and consequences of such actions.



### Subsection 8.1a: Humanitarian Intervention



One of the most commonly cited justifications for ethical intervention is humanitarian intervention. This refers to the use of military force to protect the rights and well-being of individuals within a target nation, such as in cases of genocide, ethnic cleansing, or other gross human rights violations. The philosophy of humanitarian intervention is based on the belief that all individuals have a right to basic human rights and dignity, and that it is the responsibility of the international community to protect these rights.



However, the concept of humanitarian intervention is not without its controversies. One of the main criticisms is the issue of sovereignty, as intervention by a foreign nation can be seen as a violation of a nation's right to self-determination. This raises questions about the legitimacy and authority of external actors to intervene in the affairs of another nation.



Furthermore, the decision to intervene in a humanitarian crisis is often complex and subjective, as it involves weighing the potential consequences and risks of intervention against the moral imperative to protect human rights. This can lead to debates and disagreements among nations and within the international community, as seen in the case of the 1994 Rwandan genocide where the international community failed to intervene.



In addition, the effectiveness of humanitarian intervention in achieving its intended goals has also been called into question. In some cases, intervention has led to unintended consequences and further destabilization of the target nation, as seen in the aftermath of the 2011 NATO intervention in Libya.



Despite these challenges, the concept of humanitarian intervention remains a crucial aspect of the ethics of intervention. It highlights the importance of considering the well-being and rights of individuals within a nation, and the responsibility of the international community to protect these rights when they are being violated.



In the next section, we will delve deeper into the ethical frameworks and principles that have been used to justify or condemn military intervention by great powers. This will provide a more comprehensive understanding of the complexities and nuances involved in the decision to intervene, and the potential consequences for all parties involved.





# Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



## Chapter 8: The Ethics of Intervention



### Section 8.1: Defining Ethical Intervention



In this section, we will explore the concept of ethical intervention and its various interpretations. Ethical intervention refers to the use of military force by a powerful nation to intervene in the affairs of another nation, with the intention of promoting a certain set of values or principles. This can include humanitarian intervention, where the goal is to protect the rights and well-being of individuals within the target nation, or strategic intervention, where the goal is to advance the interests of the intervening nation.



The concept of ethical intervention has a long history, with various philosophers and political thinkers offering their perspectives on its justification and limitations. One of the earliest proponents of ethical intervention was the Victorian liberal John Stuart Mill, who argued that there are cases where it is morally justifiable for a powerful nation to intervene in the affairs of a weaker nation. However, Mill's justification for intervention was rooted in overt imperialism, as he believed that "barbarous" nations could benefit from the intervention of a more civilized nation.



This idea of a "civilizing mission" has been heavily criticized in modern discourse, as it perpetuates a colonial mindset and ignores the agency and sovereignty of the target nation. However, Mill's argument highlights the importance of considering the intentions and motivations behind ethical intervention, as they can greatly impact the outcome and consequences of such actions.



### Subsection 8.1a: Humanitarian Intervention



One of the most commonly cited justifications for ethical intervention is humanitarian intervention. This refers to the use of military force to protect the rights and well-being of individuals within a target nation, such as in cases of genocide, ethnic cleansing, or other gross human rights violations. The concept of humanitarian intervention is rooted in the belief that all individuals have inherent rights that must be protected, and that it is the responsibility of the international community to intervene when these rights are being violated.



However, the concept of humanitarian intervention is not without its controversies. One of the main debates surrounding this form of intervention is the issue of sovereignty. Some argue that a nation's sovereignty should be respected and that external intervention is a violation of this principle. Others argue that the responsibility to protect human rights should take precedence over sovereignty.



### Subsection 8.1b: Just War Theory



Another important ethical framework for understanding military intervention is the theory of just war. This theory has its roots in ancient Greece and Rome, and was later developed and refined by Christian thinkers such as Augustine of Hippo. Just war theory sets out criteria for determining when a war can be considered just, both in terms of the reasons for going to war (jus ad bellum) and the conduct during war (jus in bello).



One of the key principles of just war theory is the idea of proportionality, which states that the harm caused by the war must not outweigh the potential benefits. This principle is often used to evaluate the justification for military intervention, as it requires a careful consideration of the potential consequences of intervention.



However, just war theory is not without its limitations. Some argue that it is too subjective and open to interpretation, and that it can be used to justify aggressive actions by powerful nations. Others argue that it is too focused on the conduct of war and does not adequately address the underlying causes of conflict.



In conclusion, ethical intervention is a complex and controversial topic that requires careful consideration of various ethical frameworks and principles. Whether it is through humanitarian intervention or strategic intervention, the decision to intervene in the affairs of another nation must be made with a deep understanding of the potential consequences and a commitment to upholding ethical standards.





### Subsection 8.1c: Responsibility to Protect



The concept of "Responsibility to Protect" (R2P) has gained significant attention in recent years as a potential justification for ethical intervention. R2P is a principle that was first introduced in 2005 by the United Nations World Summit, and it states that every nation has a responsibility to protect its citizens from mass atrocities, such as genocide, war crimes, ethnic cleansing, and crimes against humanity. However, if a nation is unable or unwilling to protect its citizens, then the international community has a responsibility to intervene and protect those citizens.



R2P is based on the idea that sovereignty is not an absolute right, and that the international community has a responsibility to protect individuals from grave human rights violations. This principle has been used to justify interventions in cases such as the 2011 NATO intervention in Libya, where the goal was to protect civilians from the violent repression of the Gaddafi regime.



However, the concept of R2P is not without its critics. Some argue that it is a form of neo-colonialism, as it allows powerful nations to intervene in the affairs of weaker nations under the guise of protecting human rights. Others argue that R2P is too vague and open to interpretation, and that it could be used to justify interventions for political or economic gain rather than genuine humanitarian concerns.



Despite these criticisms, R2P remains a significant concept in the discussion of ethical intervention. It highlights the importance of considering the responsibility of the international community to protect individuals from mass atrocities, while also acknowledging the potential risks and consequences of intervention. As with any justification for ethical intervention, the intentions and motivations behind R2P must be carefully examined to ensure that it is truly being used for the benefit of those in need.





### Subsection 8.2a: Sovereignty vs. Human Rights



One of the key ethical dilemmas in intervention is the tension between a nation's sovereignty and the protection of human rights. Sovereignty is the principle that a nation has the right to govern itself without interference from external forces. On the other hand, human rights are the fundamental rights and freedoms that every individual is entitled to, regardless of their nationality or citizenship.



This tension between sovereignty and human rights is particularly evident in cases where a government is committing mass atrocities against its own citizens. The international community is faced with the question of whether to respect a nation's sovereignty and refrain from intervention, or to prioritize the protection of human rights and intervene to stop the violence.



One example of this dilemma is the 1994 Rwandan genocide, where the international community failed to intervene and stop the mass killings of Tutsis by the Hutu-led government. At the time, the principle of non-intervention was seen as more important than the protection of human rights. However, in the aftermath of the genocide, there was widespread criticism of the international community's inaction and calls for a re-evaluation of the balance between sovereignty and human rights.



The concept of "Responsibility to Protect" (R2P) has emerged as a potential solution to this dilemma. R2P recognizes that sovereignty is not an absolute right and that the international community has a responsibility to protect individuals from mass atrocities. This principle was used to justify the 2011 NATO intervention in Libya, where the goal was to protect civilians from the violent repression of the Gaddafi regime.



However, the implementation of R2P has also raised ethical concerns. Some argue that it is a form of neo-colonialism, as it allows powerful nations to intervene in the affairs of weaker nations under the guise of protecting human rights. Others argue that R2P is too vague and open to interpretation, and that it could be used to justify interventions for political or economic gain rather than genuine humanitarian concerns.



In addition, the concept of R2P raises questions about the role of the international community in determining when and how to intervene. Who decides when a government is unable or unwilling to protect its citizens? How is the decision to intervene made? These questions highlight the complexities and challenges of balancing sovereignty and human rights in the context of intervention.



In conclusion, the tension between sovereignty and human rights is a significant ethical dilemma in intervention. While the concept of R2P offers a potential solution, it also raises important questions and concerns. As the international community continues to grapple with this dilemma, it is crucial to carefully consider the motivations and consequences of intervention to ensure that it is truly being used for the benefit of those in need.





### Subsection 8.2b: Selectivity of Intervention



Another ethical dilemma in intervention is the issue of selectivity. Selectivity refers to the tendency of powerful nations to intervene in conflicts that align with their own interests, while ignoring or neglecting conflicts that do not directly affect them. This raises questions about the impartiality and fairness of intervention, as well as the potential for hidden agendas and ulterior motives.



One example of selectivity in intervention is the 2003 invasion of Iraq by the United States and its allies. The justification for this intervention was the belief that Iraq possessed weapons of mass destruction, which later turned out to be false. This raised concerns about the true motives behind the intervention and whether it was driven by political and economic interests rather than a genuine concern for human rights.



Selectivity in intervention also raises questions about the consistency of international norms and standards. Why is it that some conflicts are deemed worthy of intervention while others are not? This inconsistency can undermine the legitimacy of intervention and lead to accusations of double standards.



Furthermore, selectivity can also have unintended consequences. By intervening in some conflicts and not others, powerful nations may inadvertently perpetuate or exacerbate existing power imbalances and inequalities. This can lead to further instability and conflict in the long run.



To address the issue of selectivity, it is important for the international community to establish clear and consistent criteria for intervention. This could include factors such as the severity of human rights violations, the level of threat to international peace and security, and the potential for successful intervention. Additionally, there should be a mechanism for oversight and accountability to ensure that interventions are carried out in an impartial and ethical manner.



In conclusion, the selectivity of intervention is a complex ethical dilemma that requires careful consideration and evaluation. While intervention can be a powerful tool for protecting human rights and promoting stability, it must be carried out in a consistent and impartial manner to maintain its legitimacy and effectiveness. 





### Subsection 8.2c: Unintended Consequences



In addition to the ethical dilemmas of selectivity and the potential for perverse consequences, another important consideration in intervention is the possibility of unintended consequences. These are outcomes that are not foreseen or intended by the interveners, but are a result of their actions.



One example of unintended consequences in intervention is the case of the horse stirrup, as described by historian Lynn White. The invention of this technology enabled new patterns of warfare, which eventually led to the development of feudalism. This was an unintended consequence of the stirrup, which was originally intended to improve cavalry tactics.



Similarly, many modern technologies have negative unintended consequences that are both unavoidable and unpredictable. For example, the widespread adoption of automobiles has led to traffic congestion, deaths and injuries from car accidents, air pollution, and global warming. These were not the intended consequences of the invention of the automobile, but they have become major issues in our society.



In the context of military intervention, unintended consequences can have serious and long-lasting effects. For instance, interventions aimed at promoting democracy and stability in a region may inadvertently lead to further instability and conflict. This was seen in the case of the 2003 invasion of Iraq, where the removal of Saddam Hussein's regime resulted in a power vacuum and increased sectarian violence.



Furthermore, interventions can also have unintended consequences on a global scale. For example, the use of military force in one region may have ripple effects on other regions, leading to increased tensions and conflicts. Additionally, interventions can also have unintended consequences for the environment, as seen in the case of the Gulf War, where the use of oil fires and chemical weapons had long-lasting environmental impacts.



To address the issue of unintended consequences, it is important for interveners to carefully consider the potential outcomes of their actions and to have contingency plans in place. This requires thorough research and analysis of the situation, as well as a deep understanding of the cultural, political, and social dynamics at play. Additionally, there should be a mechanism for monitoring and evaluating the effects of intervention, in order to make necessary adjustments and mitigate any unintended consequences.



In conclusion, the potential for unintended consequences is an important ethical consideration in military intervention. Interveners must carefully weigh the potential risks and consequences of their actions, and take steps to minimize any unintended negative effects. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the complex and controversial topic of the ethics of intervention in military affairs by great powers. We have discussed the various justifications and motivations for intervention, as well as the potential consequences and implications of such actions. We have also examined the role of international law and the responsibility of great powers in upholding it. Through this comprehensive study, we have gained a deeper understanding of the complexities and challenges surrounding military intervention by great powers.



As we have seen, the ethics of intervention is a highly debated and subjective topic. While some argue that it is the responsibility of great powers to intervene in order to protect human rights and promote stability, others believe that such actions are often driven by self-interest and can lead to further harm and instability. It is clear that there is no easy answer or solution to this issue, and each situation must be carefully evaluated on its own merits.



However, one thing is certain: the decision to intervene in military affairs should not be taken lightly. It is a serious and consequential action that can have far-reaching consequences, both positive and negative. Therefore, it is crucial for great powers to carefully consider all factors and implications before taking such action, and to always prioritize the well-being and rights of those affected by their intervention.

## Chapter: Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will be exploring the role of international organizations in the context of great power military intervention. International organizations, also known as intergovernmental organizations (IGOs), are formal institutions created by states to facilitate cooperation and coordination on various issues of mutual interest. These organizations play a significant role in shaping the international landscape and have a significant impact on the decisions and actions of great powers.



The use of military force by great powers has been a contentious issue in international relations, with debates surrounding the legality, morality, and effectiveness of such interventions. In recent years, there has been a growing trend towards multilateral military interventions, where multiple states, often through international organizations, come together to intervene in a conflict or crisis. This has raised questions about the role of international organizations in these interventions and their impact on the outcomes.



This chapter will provide a comprehensive study of the role of international organizations in great power military interventions. We will examine the various ways in which these organizations are involved in such interventions, including their decision-making processes, their contributions to military operations, and their role in post-conflict reconstruction and peacebuilding. We will also explore the challenges and limitations faced by international organizations in these interventions and the potential implications for the future of international relations.



Overall, this chapter aims to provide a deeper understanding of the complex dynamics between great powers and international organizations in the context of military intervention. By examining the role of international organizations in these interventions, we can gain insights into the evolving nature of international relations and the potential for cooperation and conflict resolution in the international arena. 





### Section: 9.1 United Nations and Intervention:



The United Nations (UN) is one of the most prominent international organizations in the world, with 193 member states. Its primary purpose, as stated in Article 1 of the UN Charter, is to maintain international peace and security. As such, the UN plays a crucial role in great power military interventions, which often involve issues of peace and security.



#### Subsection: 9.1a UN Charter and Intervention



The UN Charter, adopted in 1945, serves as the foundational document for the organization and outlines its principles and purposes. Article 2 of the Charter, titled "Principles of the United Nations," sets out the guidelines for how the UN and its member states should act in pursuit of its purposes. This includes the use of force and intervention in the affairs of other states.



Article 2, clauses 3-4 of the Charter prohibit the threat or use of force by member states, with the exception of self-defense. This means that any military intervention by a great power must be justified as an act of self-defense or authorized by the UN Security Council. This principle is reaffirmed in Article 51, which states that the right to self-defense is inherent and can be exercised in response to an armed attack.



Furthermore, Article 2, clauses 4-5 of the Charter also prohibit any actions that would undermine the territorial integrity or political independence of another state. This includes covert operations or support for activities that aim to destabilize a sovereign state. These clauses serve as a check on the actions of great powers and aim to prevent any unilateral interventions that could lead to further conflict.



However, Article 2, clause 7 of the Charter also recognizes the principle of non-intervention in the domestic affairs of states. This means that the UN cannot intervene in matters that are within the domestic jurisdiction of a state, unless authorized by the Security Council. This principle is crucial in maintaining the sovereignty of states and preventing any interference in their internal affairs.



The UN Charter also outlines the role of the Security Council in authorizing military interventions. Chapter VII of the Charter, titled "Action with Respect to Threats to the Peace, Breaches of the Peace, and Acts of Aggression," gives the Security Council the power to determine the existence of any threat to peace or act of aggression and to take necessary measures to maintain or restore international peace and security. This includes authorizing military interventions by member states.



In addition to the Charter, the Preamble of the UN also serves as an interpretative guide for the provisions of the Charter. It highlights the core motives of the founders of the organization, including the desire to save succeeding generations from the scourge of war and to reaffirm faith in fundamental human rights. These principles are reflected in the provisions of the Charter and guide the actions of the UN in matters of intervention.



Overall, the UN Charter and its provisions play a crucial role in shaping the involvement of the UN in great power military interventions. It serves as a framework for maintaining international peace and security and provides guidelines for the actions of member states and the UN itself. However, the effectiveness of these principles in preventing conflicts and promoting peace remains a subject of debate. 





### Section: 9.1 United Nations and Intervention:



The United Nations (UN) is one of the most prominent international organizations in the world, with 193 member states. Its primary purpose, as stated in Article 1 of the UN Charter, is to maintain international peace and security. As such, the UN plays a crucial role in great power military interventions, which often involve issues of peace and security.



#### Subsection: 9.1b UN Peacekeeping Missions



One of the key ways in which the UN carries out its mandate of maintaining international peace and security is through peacekeeping missions. These missions are deployed in areas of conflict or post-conflict situations, with the aim of promoting peace and stability. The Indian Army has been actively involved in UN peacekeeping missions, with a long history of successful contributions.



The current UN peacekeeping missions undertaken by the Indian Armed Forces include Lebanon (UNIFIL), Congo (MONUSCO), and Sudan and South Sudan (UNMIS/UNMISS). These missions involve the deployment of infantry battalion groups, medical units, military observers, and staff officers. The Indian Army has also provided a Deputy Force Commander and a Deputy Police Commissioner for the UNMIS/UNMISS mission.



The Indian Army's involvement in these missions has been crucial in maintaining peace and stability in these regions. For instance, in the Congo mission, the Indian Army's intervention brigade, along with the support of MONUSCO, was able to successfully destroy the M-23 Rebel Group. This highlights the effectiveness of UN peacekeeping missions in promoting peace and security.



However, it is important to note that UN peacekeeping missions are not without their challenges. The current situation in Lebanon, for example, is tense and volatile due to the crises in Syria. This poses a threat to the safety and security of the peacekeepers deployed in the region. In addition, the UN's mandate for these missions is renewed annually, which can lead to uncertainty and instability in the long term.



Despite these challenges, UN peacekeeping missions remain an important tool for promoting peace and security in areas of conflict. The Indian Army's active involvement in these missions showcases the country's commitment to the UN's principles and purposes. As such, the UN continues to play a crucial role in great power military interventions, with peacekeeping missions serving as a key component of this role.





### Section: 9.1 United Nations and Intervention:



The United Nations (UN) is one of the most prominent international organizations in the world, with 193 member states. Its primary purpose, as stated in Article 1 of the UN Charter, is to maintain international peace and security. As such, the UN plays a crucial role in great power military interventions, which often involve issues of peace and security.



#### Subsection: 9.1c Case Studies



The United Nations has been involved in numerous military interventions throughout its history, with varying degrees of success. In this section, we will examine two case studies of UN interventions and their outcomes.



##### Case Study 1: The Korean War



The Korean War, which lasted from 1950 to 1953, was a major conflict between North and South Korea. The war began when North Korea, supported by the Soviet Union and China, invaded South Korea. The United Nations Security Council, with the support of the United States, passed a resolution calling for a ceasefire and the withdrawal of North Korean forces.



The UN then formed a coalition of 16 countries, led by the United States, to intervene in the conflict. The UN forces were able to push back the North Korean army and regain control of South Korea. However, the intervention was not without its challenges. The Chinese army entered the war on the side of North Korea, leading to a stalemate and a long period of negotiations before a ceasefire was finally reached.



The Korean War is often seen as a successful UN intervention, as it achieved its goal of restoring peace and stability in the region. However, it also highlighted the limitations of the UN's power, as the involvement of major world powers complicated the situation and prolonged the conflict.



##### Case Study 2: The Rwandan Genocide



In 1994, the small African nation of Rwanda experienced a horrific genocide, in which an estimated 800,000 people were killed in just 100 days. The UN had a peacekeeping mission in Rwanda at the time, but it was unable to prevent the genocide from occurring.



The UN's failure to intervene in the Rwandan genocide has been heavily criticized, with many arguing that the organization did not take the situation seriously enough and did not provide enough resources to the peacekeeping mission. This case highlights the challenges and limitations of UN interventions, particularly in situations where there is a lack of political will or resources.



Despite these challenges, the UN continues to play a crucial role in maintaining international peace and security through its peacekeeping missions and other interventions. However, it is important to recognize the limitations of the organization and work towards improving its effectiveness in future interventions.





### Section: 9.2 Regional Organizations and Intervention:



Regional organizations play a significant role in great power military interventions, as they often have a better understanding of the local dynamics and can provide more targeted support. In this section, we will examine the role of NATO in military interventions.



#### Subsection: 9.2a NATO and Intervention



NATO, or the North Atlantic Treaty Organization, is a military alliance of 30 countries in North America and Europe. It was formed in 1949 as a collective defense against the Soviet Union during the Cold War. While its primary purpose is to ensure the security of its member states, NATO has also been involved in several military interventions.



One of the most notable interventions by NATO was during the Kosovo War in 1999. The conflict arose from tensions between the Albanian majority and Serbian minority in Kosovo, a province of Serbia. As the situation escalated, NATO launched a 78-day air campaign against Serbian forces, which eventually led to the withdrawal of Serbian troops from Kosovo and the establishment of a UN administration in the region.



NATO's intervention in Kosovo was controversial, as it was not authorized by the UN Security Council and was seen by some as a violation of international law. However, it also demonstrated the organization's willingness to take action in the face of humanitarian crises and its ability to effectively carry out military operations.



In recent years, NATO has also been involved in interventions in Afghanistan and Libya. In Afghanistan, NATO led a coalition of forces to combat terrorism and support the Afghan government. In Libya, NATO enforced a no-fly zone and carried out airstrikes in support of rebel forces during the Libyan Civil War.



However, NATO's involvement in these interventions has also raised questions about its effectiveness and the potential for mission creep. In Afghanistan, despite nearly two decades of military presence, the country remains unstable and the Taliban continues to pose a threat. In Libya, the intervention led to the overthrow of Muammar Gaddafi, but also resulted in a power vacuum and ongoing conflict.



Overall, NATO's role in military interventions highlights the complex nature of international organizations and their involvement in conflicts. While they can provide valuable support and resources, their actions must also be carefully considered and in line with international law. 





### Section: 9.2 Regional Organizations and Intervention:



Regional organizations play a crucial role in great power military interventions, as they have a better understanding of the local dynamics and can provide more targeted support. In this section, we will examine the role of the African Union (AU) in military interventions.



#### Subsection: 9.2b African Union and Intervention



The African Union (AU) is a regional organization consisting of 55 member states in Africa. It was formed in 2002 as a successor to the Organization of African Unity (OAU) with the goal of promoting unity, peace, and development in Africa. The AU has been actively involved in addressing conflicts and crises in the continent, making it a key player in regional interventions.



One of the most significant interventions by the AU was in the Darfur region of Sudan. In 2004, the AU deployed a peacekeeping mission, the African Union Mission in Sudan (AMIS), to monitor the ceasefire agreement between the Sudanese government and rebel groups. The mission was later replaced by the United Nations-African Union Hybrid Operation in Darfur (UNAMID) in 2007. The AU's involvement in Darfur demonstrated its commitment to resolving conflicts and promoting peace in the region.



The AU has also been involved in interventions in Somalia, Mali, and the Central African Republic. In Somalia, the AU has deployed a peacekeeping mission, the African Union Mission in Somalia (AMISOM), to support the Somali government in its fight against militant groups. In Mali, the AU has supported the United Nations Multidimensional Integrated Stabilization Mission in Mali (MINUSMA) in its efforts to stabilize the country. In the Central African Republic, the AU has deployed a peacekeeping mission, the African Union Mission in the Central African Republic (MISCA), to protect civilians and support the transitional government.



The AU's interventions have not been without challenges. Limited resources, lack of coordination among member states, and political divisions have hindered the effectiveness of AU missions. However, the AU has shown a willingness to take action in the face of humanitarian crises and has made efforts to improve its peacekeeping capabilities.



The AU's involvement in interventions raises questions about the role of regional organizations in international interventions. Some argue that regional organizations are better equipped to understand the local context and have a more legitimate presence in the region. Others argue that regional organizations may have their own political agendas and may not always act in the best interest of the affected countries.



In conclusion, the African Union plays a significant role in regional interventions in Africa. Its involvement in conflicts and crises demonstrates its commitment to promoting peace and stability in the continent. However, challenges remain, and further efforts are needed to strengthen the AU's peacekeeping capabilities and coordination among member states. 





### Section: 9.2 Regional Organizations and Intervention:



Regional organizations play a crucial role in great power military interventions, as they have a better understanding of the local dynamics and can provide more targeted support. In this section, we will examine the role of the Association of Southeast Asian Nations (ASEAN) in military interventions.



#### Subsection: 9.2c ASEAN and Intervention



The Association of Southeast Asian Nations (ASEAN) is a regional organization consisting of ten member states in Southeast Asia. It was formed in 1967 with the goal of promoting economic growth, social progress, and cultural development in the region. However, in recent years, ASEAN has also played a significant role in addressing security issues and conflicts in the region.



One of the most notable interventions by ASEAN was in the Cambodian conflict in the 1980s. ASEAN, along with other regional organizations, played a crucial role in facilitating negotiations between the warring parties and eventually helped to bring about a peaceful resolution to the conflict.



ASEAN has also been actively involved in addressing the issue of piracy in the region. With five of its member states having concerns with piracy, ASEAN has taken steps to enhance maritime security and cooperation among its members. This includes joint patrols and intelligence sharing to combat piracy in the region.



In addition, ASEAN has also been working towards countering the threat of Islamic terrorism in the region. The organization has held annual intelligence summits to enhance cooperation and information sharing among its members. This has been particularly important for countries like Indonesia, Malaysia, and the Philippines, which have faced significant challenges from terrorist groups.



However, ASEAN's interventions have not been without challenges. Limited resources and differing priorities among its members have often hindered the organization's ability to take decisive action in addressing security issues. Furthermore, ASEAN's principle of non-interference in the internal affairs of its member states has also been a barrier to more robust interventions.



Despite these challenges, ASEAN's role in military interventions in the region cannot be overlooked. The organization's efforts have contributed to maintaining peace and stability in Southeast Asia, and its continued cooperation and coordination will be crucial in addressing future security challenges. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the role of international organizations in great power military intervention. We have seen that these organizations play a crucial role in shaping the decisions and actions of great powers when it comes to military intervention. From providing a platform for diplomatic discussions to coordinating and implementing peacekeeping missions, international organizations have a significant impact on the outcomes of military interventions.



One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of multilateralism in great power military intervention. International organizations provide a forum for multiple countries to come together and discuss potential interventions, allowing for a more balanced and comprehensive approach. This not only increases the chances of success but also helps to mitigate the negative consequences of unilateral interventions.



Another important aspect highlighted in this chapter is the need for effective communication and cooperation between international organizations and great powers. Without proper coordination and collaboration, military interventions can become chaotic and ineffective. Therefore, it is crucial for international organizations to maintain strong relationships with great powers and work together towards common goals.



Overall, this chapter has shown that international organizations are essential players in the realm of great power military intervention. Their involvement can greatly influence the success or failure of interventions and their role should not be underestimated.

Discuss the advantages and disadvantages of multilateralism in great power military intervention. Provide examples of successful and unsuccessful multilateral interventions.



#### Exercise 2: The Role of Communication in Military Intervention

Explain the importance of effective communication between international organizations and great powers in military intervention. How can miscommunication lead to negative outcomes?

## Chapter: Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



### Introduction



In the previous chapters, we have explored the various aspects of great power military intervention, including its historical context, motivations, and consequences. However, one crucial aspect that has not been thoroughly examined is the role of non-state actors in these interventions. Non-state actors, also known as non-governmental organizations (NGOs), are entities that operate independently of any government and often have a specific agenda or goal. In this chapter, we will delve into the complex relationship between great powers and non-state actors in the context of military intervention.



Non-state actors have played a significant role in shaping the landscape of international relations, especially in the realm of military intervention. They can range from humanitarian organizations providing aid in conflict zones to terrorist groups seeking to destabilize governments. These actors often have their own interests and agendas, which may align or conflict with those of great powers. As such, their involvement in military interventions can have a significant impact on the outcome and effectiveness of these interventions.



This chapter will explore the various types of non-state actors involved in military interventions, their motivations, and their methods of operation. We will also examine the ways in which great powers interact with these actors and the implications of such interactions. Additionally, we will analyze case studies of past military interventions to understand the role of non-state actors in shaping the outcome of these interventions.



Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the role of non-state actors in great power military intervention. By examining the complexities of this relationship, we can gain valuable insights into the dynamics of international relations and the impact of non-state actors on global security. 





## Chapter: Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



### Introduction



In the previous chapters, we have explored the various aspects of great power military intervention, including its historical context, motivations, and consequences. However, one crucial aspect that has not been thoroughly examined is the role of non-state actors in these interventions. Non-state actors, also known as non-governmental organizations (NGOs), are entities that operate independently of any government and often have a specific agenda or goal. In this chapter, we will delve into the complex relationship between great powers and non-state actors in the context of military intervention.



Non-state actors have played a significant role in shaping the landscape of international relations, especially in the realm of military intervention. They can range from humanitarian organizations providing aid in conflict zones to terrorist groups seeking to destabilize governments. These actors often have their own interests and agendas, which may align or conflict with those of great powers. As such, their involvement in military interventions can have a significant impact on the outcome and effectiveness of these interventions.



This chapter will explore the various types of non-state actors involved in military interventions, their motivations, and their methods of operation. We will also examine the ways in which great powers interact with these actors and the implications of such interactions. Additionally, we will analyze case studies of past military interventions to understand the role of non-state actors in shaping the outcome of these interventions.



Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the role of non-state actors in great power military intervention. By examining the complexities of this relationship, we can gain valuable insights into the dynamics of international relations and the impact of non-state actors on global security. 



### Section: 10.1 NGOs and Intervention



Non-governmental organizations (NGOs) have become increasingly involved in military interventions, playing a significant role in shaping the outcome of these interventions. In this section, we will explore the various types of NGOs involved in military interventions and their motivations.



#### Subsection: 10.1a Humanitarian NGOs



One type of NGO that is commonly involved in military interventions is humanitarian NGOs. These organizations provide aid and assistance to communities affected by conflict or natural disasters. The International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement is a prime example of a humanitarian NGO that operates globally.



Humanitarian NGOs are guided by the principles of humanity, impartiality, and independence, which stress the importance of providing aid based on need alone. These principles are derived from the Fundamental Principles of the International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement. However, the principle of neutrality, which is directly related to the special international role of the Red Cross, is not included in the code of conduct. This is because organizations such as military forces and for-profit companies may also provide assistance in disaster zones, but they do not necessarily adhere to the core principles of humanitarian NGOs.



The voluntary nature of the code of conduct for humanitarian NGOs allows for self-enforcement by each signatory organization. This code also includes recommendations for both disaster-affected countries and donor governments, highlighting the importance of cooperation between NGOs and governments in humanitarian interventions.



The involvement of humanitarian NGOs in military interventions can have a significant impact on the outcome of these interventions. Their presence can provide much-needed aid and assistance to affected communities, but it can also complicate the situation by introducing additional actors with their own agendas. In the next section, we will explore the implications of NGO involvement in military interventions through case studies.





## Chapter: - Chapter 10: The Role of Non-State Actors:



### Section: - Section: 10.1 NGOs and Intervention:



### Subsection (optional): 10.1b Advocacy NGOs



Non-state actors, also known as non-governmental organizations (NGOs), play a crucial role in shaping the landscape of international relations, particularly in the context of military intervention. These organizations operate independently of any government and often have a specific agenda or goal. In this section, we will focus on one type of NGO - advocacy NGOs - and their role in great power military intervention.



Advocacy NGOs are organizations that work to promote a specific cause or issue, often through lobbying and campaigning efforts. These NGOs can range from human rights organizations to environmental groups, and their involvement in military intervention can have significant implications.



One of the key strengths of advocacy NGOs is their ability to mobilize resources and raise awareness about issues that may not receive enough attention from governments or international organizations. In the context of military intervention, these NGOs can play a crucial role in advocating for the protection of civilians and promoting human rights. For example, during the Syrian civil war, organizations such as Amnesty International and Human Rights Watch documented human rights abuses and called for international intervention to protect civilians.



Advocacy NGOs also have the power to influence public opinion and shape the narrative surrounding military intervention. Through their campaigns and media presence, these organizations can sway public opinion in favor of or against intervention. This can have a significant impact on the decisions made by great powers regarding military intervention.



However, the involvement of advocacy NGOs in military intervention is not without its challenges. These organizations often face criticism for being biased and pursuing their own agendas. In some cases, their actions may even hinder the effectiveness of military intervention. For example, during the NATO intervention in Kosovo, some NGOs were accused of exaggerating the number of civilian casualties, which led to a delay in the intervention and ultimately affected its success.



Despite these challenges, advocacy NGOs continue to play a crucial role in shaping the discourse and outcomes of great power military intervention. Their ability to mobilize resources, raise awareness, and influence public opinion makes them valuable partners for great powers in achieving their goals. However, it is essential to recognize and address the potential biases and limitations of these organizations to ensure the effectiveness of military intervention. 





## Chapter: - Chapter 10: The Role of Non-State Actors:



### Section: - Section: 10.1 NGOs and Intervention:



### Subsection (optional): 10.1c Challenges and Critiques



While advocacy NGOs play a crucial role in shaping the landscape of international relations, their involvement in military intervention is not without its challenges and critiques. In this subsection, we will discuss some of the main challenges and criticisms faced by advocacy NGOs in the context of military intervention.



One of the main challenges faced by advocacy NGOs is the issue of bias. These organizations often have a specific agenda or goal, which can lead to a biased perspective on the situation at hand. This bias can be influenced by factors such as funding sources, political affiliations, and personal beliefs of the organization's members. As a result, their actions and statements may not always be objective and may be perceived as pushing a particular agenda.



Another criticism of advocacy NGOs is their lack of accountability. Unlike governments or international organizations, NGOs are not held accountable for their actions and decisions. This lack of accountability can lead to a lack of transparency and can raise questions about the credibility of their claims and actions. In some cases, NGOs may also face accusations of exaggerating or fabricating information to further their agenda.



Moreover, the involvement of advocacy NGOs in military intervention can also lead to the politicization of humanitarian issues. By advocating for a specific cause or issue, these organizations may be seen as taking sides in a conflict, which can further complicate the situation. This can also lead to a lack of trust from all parties involved, making it challenging for NGOs to effectively carry out their work.



Lastly, the involvement of advocacy NGOs in military intervention can also have unintended consequences. For example, their campaigns and media presence may lead to an increase in public pressure for intervention, which can influence the decisions of great powers. However, this pressure may not always align with the best interests of the affected population or the long-term stability of the region.



In conclusion, while advocacy NGOs play a crucial role in promoting human rights and raising awareness about issues that may not receive enough attention, their involvement in military intervention is not without its challenges and criticisms. It is essential for these organizations to maintain transparency and accountability and to consider the potential consequences of their actions in order to effectively contribute to the resolution of conflicts.





## Chapter: - Chapter 10: The Role of Non-State Actors:



### Section: - Section: 10.2 Private Military Companies and Intervention:



### Subsection (optional): 10.2a Definition and Role



Private military companies (PMCs) are private companies that provide military and security services to governments, international organizations, and other non-state actors. These companies have become increasingly prevalent in recent years, with their involvement in military intervention raising questions about their role and impact.



PMCs are often hired by governments or international organizations to provide services such as training, logistics, and security support. They may also be contracted to carry out specific military operations, such as providing armed personnel for peacekeeping missions or conducting intelligence gathering. In some cases, PMCs may even be hired to provide direct combat support.



The role of PMCs in military intervention is a controversial topic, with both supporters and critics voicing their opinions. Supporters argue that PMCs offer a cost-effective and efficient alternative to traditional military forces. They also argue that PMCs can provide specialized skills and expertise that may not be available within a country's own military.



On the other hand, critics raise concerns about the lack of accountability and transparency of PMCs. As private companies, they are not subject to the same regulations and oversight as traditional military forces. This can lead to issues such as human rights abuses and the use of excessive force. Critics also argue that the use of PMCs can undermine the sovereignty of a country and lead to the privatization of war.



The involvement of PMCs in military intervention also raises questions about the ethics and morality of outsourcing military operations to private companies. Some argue that this practice goes against the principles of just war theory, which states that the use of force should only be carried out by legitimate authorities. Others argue that the profit motive of PMCs may lead to conflicts of interest and compromise the objectives of the intervention.



Despite these concerns, PMCs continue to play a significant role in military intervention. As the demand for their services increases, it is essential to carefully consider the implications of their involvement and ensure proper oversight and accountability measures are in place. 





## Chapter: - Chapter 10: The Role of Non-State Actors:



### Section: - Section: 10.2 Private Military Companies and Intervention:



### Subsection (optional): 10.2b Ethical and Legal Issues



Private military companies (PMCs) have become increasingly prevalent in modern military intervention, raising ethical and legal concerns. As private companies, they operate outside of traditional military structures and are not subject to the same regulations and oversight. This can lead to issues such as human rights abuses and the use of excessive force, as well as concerns about the privatization of war and the erosion of national sovereignty.



One of the main ethical concerns surrounding PMCs is their lack of accountability and transparency. Unlike traditional military forces, PMCs are not subject to the same rules of engagement and are not held to the same standards of conduct. This can lead to a lack of oversight and potential abuses of power. For example, in the Buried Bodies Case, a PMC was accused of committing war crimes while operating in a conflict zone. Due to their status as a private company, they were not subject to the same legal consequences as a traditional military force would be.



Another ethical issue is the potential for conflicts of interest. PMCs are for-profit companies, and their primary goal is to make money. This can create a conflict between their financial interests and the best interests of the country or organization they are contracted by. For example, a PMC may be incentivized to prolong a conflict in order to continue receiving payment for their services.



The use of PMCs also raises concerns about the morality of outsourcing military operations to private companies. This goes against the principles of just war theory, which states that the use of force should only be carried out by legitimate authorities. By outsourcing military operations, the responsibility for the use of force is shifted from the government to a private company, potentially undermining the legitimacy of the intervention.



From a legal perspective, the use of PMCs also raises questions about the application of international humanitarian law. As private companies, PMCs are not considered state actors and are not subject to the same legal obligations as traditional military forces. This can create a legal grey area, as PMCs may not be held accountable for their actions under international law.



To address these ethical and legal concerns, there have been calls for increased regulation and oversight of PMCs. This could include requiring PMCs to adhere to the same rules of engagement as traditional military forces, as well as implementing stricter licensing and monitoring processes. Additionally, there have been proposals for the creation of an international regulatory body to oversee the actions of PMCs and hold them accountable for any violations of international law.



In conclusion, the use of PMCs in military intervention raises significant ethical and legal issues. As private companies, they operate outside of traditional military structures and are not subject to the same regulations and oversight. This can lead to potential abuses of power and conflicts of interest, as well as challenges in applying international law. It is important for governments and international organizations to carefully consider the implications of using PMCs and to implement measures to ensure their actions are in line with ethical and legal standards.





## Chapter: - Chapter 10: The Role of Non-State Actors:



### Section: - Section: 10.2 Private Military Companies and Intervention:



### Subsection (optional): 10.2c Case Studies



Private military companies (PMCs) have become increasingly prevalent in modern military intervention, raising ethical and legal concerns. As private companies, they operate outside of traditional military structures and are not subject to the same regulations and oversight. This can lead to issues such as human rights abuses and the use of excessive force, as well as concerns about the privatization of war and the erosion of national sovereignty.



One of the most well-known case studies involving PMCs is the Blackwater Incident in Iraq. In 2007, employees of the private security company Blackwater were involved in a shooting in Baghdad that resulted in the deaths of 17 civilians. This incident sparked international outrage and raised questions about the accountability and oversight of PMCs.



Another case study is the involvement of PMCs in the conflict in Syria. In 2014, it was reported that the US government had hired a private company, Academi (formerly known as Blackwater), to train Syrian rebels in their fight against the Assad regime. This raised concerns about the use of private companies in sensitive and complex conflicts, as well as the potential for these companies to have their own agendas and motivations.



The use of PMCs in the conflict in Ukraine is also a notable case study. In 2014, it was reported that Russian PMCs were operating in eastern Ukraine, supporting pro-Russian separatists. This raised concerns about the use of PMCs as proxies in conflicts between great powers, and the potential for these companies to escalate tensions and prolong conflicts.



These case studies highlight the ethical and legal issues surrounding the use of PMCs in military intervention. They also demonstrate the need for greater accountability and oversight of these companies, as well as the need for clear regulations and guidelines for their use in conflicts. As the use of PMCs continues to increase, it is important for policymakers and military leaders to carefully consider the potential consequences and implications of their involvement in military operations.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the role of non-state actors in great power military intervention. We have seen that these actors, such as rebel groups, terrorist organizations, and private military companies, can have a significant impact on the outcome of interventions. They can either support or hinder the efforts of great powers, and their involvement can complicate the already complex dynamics of military intervention.



We have also discussed the motivations behind the actions of non-state actors. These can range from ideological beliefs to financial gain, and understanding these motivations is crucial in predicting their behavior and potential impact on interventions. Additionally, we have examined the strategies that great powers can use to engage with non-state actors, such as co-optation, containment, and direct military action.



Overall, it is clear that non-state actors play a crucial role in great power military intervention. Their actions and motivations must be carefully considered and addressed in order to achieve successful outcomes. Ignoring their presence or underestimating their influence can have detrimental consequences for interventions.
