## Chapter: Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will be examining a specific case study of military intervention by great powers in the country of Syria. This case study will provide a comprehensive analysis of the various factors that led to the intervention, the actions taken by the intervening powers, and the consequences of their involvement. The conflict in Syria has been ongoing since 2011 and has involved multiple actors, making it a complex and highly relevant case to study in the context of great power military intervention.



Throughout history, great powers have often intervened in the affairs of other nations for various reasons, such as protecting their own interests, promoting their ideologies, or maintaining global stability. These interventions have had significant impacts on the countries involved, as well as the international community as a whole. By examining the case of Syria, we can gain a deeper understanding of the motivations, strategies, and outcomes of great power military intervention.



This chapter will be divided into several sections, each focusing on a different aspect of the intervention in Syria. We will begin by providing a brief overview of the conflict and the key players involved. Then, we will delve into the reasons behind the intervention, including the political, economic, and humanitarian factors that influenced the decision of great powers to intervene. Next, we will analyze the actions taken by the intervening powers, including military operations, diplomatic efforts, and humanitarian aid. Finally, we will discuss the consequences of the intervention, both for Syria and the international community, and evaluate the effectiveness of the intervention in achieving its goals.



Through this case study, we hope to shed light on the complexities and challenges of great power military intervention and provide insights into the potential impacts of such interventions on both the intervening powers and the countries they intervene in. By understanding the dynamics of this particular case, we can gain valuable lessons for future interventions and contribute to the ongoing discussions on the role of great powers in global conflicts. 





### Section: 14.1 Background:



The conflict in Syria, which began in 2011, has been one of the most complex and devastating conflicts in recent history. It has involved multiple actors, including the Syrian government, rebel groups, terrorist organizations, and foreign powers. The conflict has resulted in hundreds of thousands of deaths, millions of displaced people, and widespread destruction of infrastructure and cities.



#### 14.1a Historical Context



To understand the intervention in Syria, it is important to examine the historical context of the country. Syria has a long history of foreign intervention, dating back to the Ottoman Empire and the French mandate in the early 20th century. After gaining independence in 1946, Syria experienced a series of coups and political instability, leading to the rise of the Ba'ath Party and the establishment of a socialist government in 1963.



In 1970, Hafez al-Assad seized power in a coup and established a dictatorship that lasted until his death in 2000. His son, Bashar al-Assad, succeeded him and continued the authoritarian rule. Under the Assad regime, Syria experienced economic growth and stability, but at the cost of political repression and human rights violations.



In 2011, inspired by the Arab Spring, protests erupted in Syria calling for political reform and an end to the Assad regime. The government responded with violence, leading to the escalation of the conflict and the involvement of external actors.



#### 14.1b Key Players in the Conflict



The conflict in Syria involves a complex web of actors, each with their own interests and agendas. The key players include:



- Syrian government: Led by President Bashar al-Assad, the Syrian government has been fighting to maintain control and suppress the rebellion.

- Rebel groups: These include a variety of armed groups, ranging from moderate opposition forces to extremist organizations such as ISIS and al-Qaeda.

- Foreign powers: Several countries have been involved in the conflict, either directly or indirectly, including Russia, Iran, Turkey, and the United States.

- Humanitarian organizations: As the conflict has resulted in a humanitarian crisis, various organizations have been providing aid and assistance to those affected by the conflict.



### Subsection: 14.2 Reasons for Intervention



The decision of great powers to intervene in Syria was influenced by a combination of political, economic, and humanitarian factors.



#### 14.2a Political Motivations



One of the main political motivations for intervention in Syria was the desire to protect national interests. For example, Russia has a long-standing alliance with the Syrian government and has a naval base in the country. By intervening, Russia aimed to maintain its influence in the region and protect its strategic interests.



Similarly, the United States and its allies were concerned about the spread of terrorism and the potential threat to their national security. The presence of extremist groups such as ISIS in Syria posed a significant risk, and intervention was seen as a way to combat this threat.



#### 14.2b Economic Interests



Economic interests also played a role in the decision to intervene in Syria. The country is strategically located and has valuable resources, including oil and gas. By intervening, foreign powers could gain control over these resources and secure economic benefits for themselves.



#### 14.2c Humanitarian Concerns



The humanitarian crisis in Syria was a major factor in the decision to intervene. The conflict has resulted in widespread displacement, food and water shortages, and a lack of access to healthcare. The intervention was seen as a way to protect civilians and provide aid to those in need.



### Subsection: 14.3 Actions Taken by Intervening Powers



The actions taken by the intervening powers in Syria have been varied and have evolved over time. These actions can be broadly categorized into military operations, diplomatic efforts, and humanitarian aid.



#### 14.3a Military Operations



The most significant action taken by intervening powers was the use of military force. Russia and Iran provided support to the Syrian government, while the United States and its allies conducted airstrikes against ISIS and other extremist groups. These military operations have resulted in significant casualties and have further escalated the conflict.



#### 14.3b Diplomatic Efforts



In addition to military operations, there have been various diplomatic efforts to resolve the conflict in Syria. These include peace talks and negotiations between the Syrian government and rebel groups, as well as international efforts led by the United Nations. However, these efforts have been largely unsuccessful in bringing an end to the conflict.



#### 14.3c Humanitarian Aid



Humanitarian aid has also been provided by intervening powers and various organizations. This aid has included food, water, medical supplies, and shelter for those affected by the conflict. However, the delivery of aid has been hindered by the ongoing violence and political complexities in the region.



### Subsection: 14.4 Consequences of Intervention



The intervention in Syria has had significant consequences, both for the country and the international community.



#### 14.4a Impact on Syria



The intervention has resulted in a prolonged and devastating conflict in Syria, leading to widespread destruction and loss of life. It has also exacerbated existing political and social divisions within the country. The intervention has also had a significant impact on the economy, with the destruction of infrastructure and the displacement of millions of people.



#### 14.4b International Implications



The intervention in Syria has had implications for the international community as well. It has strained relations between countries involved in the conflict, such as the United States and Russia. It has also raised concerns about the use of military force and the responsibility to protect civilians in situations of conflict.



### Conclusion



The intervention in Syria has been a complex and multifaceted case study of great power military intervention. It has highlighted the various motivations, actions, and consequences of such interventions and has raised important questions about the role of external actors in conflicts. As the conflict in Syria continues, it is crucial to continue studying and analyzing the intervention to gain a deeper understanding of its impacts and implications.





### Section: 14.1 Background:



The conflict in Syria, which began in 2011, has been one of the most complex and devastating conflicts in recent history. It has involved multiple actors, including the Syrian government, rebel groups, terrorist organizations, and foreign powers. The conflict has resulted in hundreds of thousands of deaths, millions of displaced people, and widespread destruction of infrastructure and cities.



#### 14.1a Historical Context



To understand the intervention in Syria, it is important to examine the historical context of the country. Syria has a long history of foreign intervention, dating back to the Ottoman Empire and the French mandate in the early 20th century. After gaining independence in 1946, Syria experienced a series of coups and political instability, leading to the rise of the Ba'ath Party and the establishment of a socialist government in 1963.



In 1970, Hafez al-Assad seized power in a coup and established a dictatorship that lasted until his death in 2000. His son, Bashar al-Assad, succeeded him and continued the authoritarian rule. Under the Assad regime, Syria experienced economic growth and stability, but at the cost of political repression and human rights violations.



In 2011, inspired by the Arab Spring, protests erupted in Syria calling for political reform and an end to the Assad regime. The government responded with violence, leading to the escalation of the conflict and the involvement of external actors.



#### 14.1b Key Players in the Conflict



The conflict in Syria involves a complex web of actors, each with their own interests and agendas. The key players include:



- Syrian government: Led by President Bashar al-Assad, the Syrian government has been fighting to maintain control and suppress the rebellion.

- Rebel groups: These include a variety of armed groups, ranging from moderate opposition forces to extremist organizations such as ISIS and al-Qaeda.

- Foreign powers: Several countries have been involved in the conflict, either directly or indirectly. The most prominent foreign powers include Russia, Iran, and the United States. Russia and Iran have been supporting the Assad regime, while the US has been providing aid to rebel groups.

- Regional powers: The conflict in Syria has also drawn in neighboring countries, such as Turkey, Jordan, and Israel. These countries have been impacted by the spillover of the conflict and have also been involved in providing aid to different sides.

- Terrorist organizations: The rise of extremist groups, such as ISIS and al-Qaeda, has further complicated the conflict in Syria. These groups have their own agendas and have been responsible for numerous atrocities and human rights violations.

- International organizations: The United Nations and other international organizations have been involved in efforts to find a peaceful resolution to the conflict and provide humanitarian aid to those affected by the conflict. However, their efforts have been hindered by the complex nature of the conflict and the involvement of various actors with conflicting interests.



The involvement of these different actors has made the conflict in Syria a complex and multi-faceted issue. In the next section, we will focus on the specific case of military intervention in Syria and its impact on the conflict.





### Section: 14.1 Background:



The conflict in Syria, which began in 2011, has been one of the most complex and devastating conflicts in recent history. It has involved multiple actors, including the Syrian government, rebel groups, terrorist organizations, and foreign powers. The conflict has resulted in hundreds of thousands of deaths, millions of displaced people, and widespread destruction of infrastructure and cities.



#### 14.1a Historical Context



To understand the intervention in Syria, it is important to examine the historical context of the country. Syria has a long history of foreign intervention, dating back to the Ottoman Empire and the French mandate in the early 20th century. After gaining independence in 1946, Syria experienced a series of coups and political instability, leading to the rise of the Ba'ath Party and the establishment of a socialist government in 1963.



In 1970, Hafez al-Assad seized power in a coup and established a dictatorship that lasted until his death in 2000. His son, Bashar al-Assad, succeeded him and continued the authoritarian rule. Under the Assad regime, Syria experienced economic growth and stability, but at the cost of political repression and human rights violations.



In 2011, inspired by the Arab Spring, protests erupted in Syria calling for political reform and an end to the Assad regime. The government responded with violence, leading to the escalation of the conflict and the involvement of external actors.



#### 14.1b Key Players in the Conflict



The conflict in Syria involves a complex web of actors, each with their own interests and agendas. The key players include:



- Syrian government: Led by President Bashar al-Assad, the Syrian government has been fighting to maintain control and suppress the rebellion.

- Rebel groups: These include a variety of armed groups, ranging from moderate opposition forces to extremist organizations such as ISIS and al-Qaeda.

- Foreign powers: Several countries have been involved in the conflict, either directly or indirectly. The most prominent are Russia, Iran, and Turkey, who have supported the Syrian government, and the United States, Saudi Arabia, and other Gulf states, who have supported the rebels.

- International organizations: The United Nations and its agencies, such as the World Health Organization, have been involved in providing aid and humanitarian assistance to the people affected by the conflict.

- Non-governmental organizations (NGOs): Various NGOs have also been involved in providing aid and assistance to the people affected by the conflict.



### Subsection: 14.1c International Response



The conflict in Syria has received significant attention from the international community, with various responses and actions taken to address the situation. The following are some of the key international responses to the conflict:



- United Nations Security Council Resolutions: The UN Security Council has passed several resolutions related to the conflict in Syria, including resolutions calling for a ceasefire, the delivery of humanitarian aid, and the establishment of a political transition process.

- Humanitarian aid and assistance: The United Nations and various NGOs have provided aid and assistance to the people affected by the conflict, including food, shelter, and medical supplies.

- Military intervention: Some countries, such as the United States, have conducted airstrikes against ISIS targets in Syria. Russia has also conducted airstrikes in support of the Syrian government.

- Diplomatic efforts: Various countries and international organizations have engaged in diplomatic efforts to find a resolution to the conflict, including peace talks and negotiations.

- Sanctions: The United States and the European Union have imposed economic sanctions on the Syrian government and its allies in an attempt to pressure them to end the conflict.

- Refugee resettlement: Many countries have opened their doors to Syrian refugees, providing them with a safe haven and a chance to rebuild their lives.



Despite these efforts, the conflict in Syria continues to rage on, with no clear end in sight. The international response has been criticized for being inadequate and ineffective in bringing about a resolution to the conflict. However, it is clear that international cooperation and coordination are crucial in addressing complex conflicts such as the one in Syria. 





### Section: 14.2 Analysis of Intervention:



The intervention in Syria has been a highly complex and controversial issue, with multiple actors and interests at play. In this section, we will analyze the various actors involved in the intervention and their respective interests.



#### 14.2a Actors and Interests



The key actors in the intervention in Syria include the Syrian government, rebel groups, and foreign powers. Each of these actors has their own interests and motivations for their involvement in the conflict.



The Syrian government, led by President Bashar al-Assad, has been fighting to maintain control and suppress the rebellion. Their primary interest is to maintain their power and authority over the country. The Assad regime has a long history of authoritarian rule and has been accused of human rights violations and political repression. The government's actions in response to the protests in 2011, including the use of violence, have only further solidified their desire to maintain control.



On the other hand, rebel groups in Syria have been fighting against the Assad regime in an attempt to overthrow the government and establish a new political system. These groups range from moderate opposition forces to extremist organizations such as ISIS and al-Qaeda. Their interests vary depending on their ideologies and goals, but they all share the common goal of removing Assad from power.



Foreign powers have also played a significant role in the intervention in Syria. Countries such as the United States, Russia, Turkey, and Iran have all been involved in the conflict, each with their own interests and motivations. The US and its allies have supported rebel groups in an attempt to overthrow Assad and promote democracy in the region. Russia, on the other hand, has been a staunch supporter of the Assad regime and has provided military aid to the government. Turkey and Iran have also been involved in the conflict, with Turkey supporting rebel groups and Iran supporting the Assad regime.



The interests of these foreign powers vary, but they all have a stake in the outcome of the conflict. The US and its allies see the removal of Assad as a way to promote democracy and stability in the region. Russia, on the other hand, sees the Assad regime as an ally and wants to maintain their influence in the region. Turkey and Iran also have their own strategic interests in the conflict, including protecting their borders and promoting their own agendas in the region.



In addition to these key actors, there are also other groups involved in the intervention, such as terrorist organizations like ISIS and al-Qaeda. These groups have their own interests and motivations, which often involve spreading their ideologies and gaining power in the region.



Overall, the intervention in Syria has been a complex and multi-faceted issue, with various actors and interests at play. Understanding these actors and their motivations is crucial in analyzing the intervention and its impact on the conflict. 





### Section: 14.2 Analysis of Intervention:



The intervention in Syria has been a highly complex and controversial issue, with multiple actors and interests at play. In this section, we will analyze the various actors involved in the intervention and their respective interests.



#### 14.2a Actors and Interests



The key actors in the intervention in Syria include the Syrian government, rebel groups, and foreign powers. Each of these actors has their own interests and motivations for their involvement in the conflict.



The Syrian government, led by President Bashar al-Assad, has been fighting to maintain control and suppress the rebellion. Their primary interest is to maintain their power and authority over the country. The Assad regime has a long history of authoritarian rule and has been accused of human rights violations and political repression. The government's actions in response to the protests in 2011, including the use of violence, have only further solidified their desire to maintain control.



On the other hand, rebel groups in Syria have been fighting against the Assad regime in an attempt to overthrow the government and establish a new political system. These groups range from moderate opposition forces to extremist organizations such as ISIS and al-Qaeda. Their interests vary depending on their ideologies and goals, but they all share the common goal of removing Assad from power.



Foreign powers have also played a significant role in the intervention in Syria. Countries such as the United States, Russia, Turkey, and Iran have all been involved in the conflict, each with their own interests and motivations. The US and its allies have supported rebel groups in an attempt to overthrow Assad and promote democracy in the region. Russia, on the other hand, has been a staunch supporter of the Assad regime and has provided military aid to the government. Turkey and Iran have also been involved in the conflict, with Turkey supporting rebel groups and Iran supporting the Assad regime.



#### 14.2b Strategies and Tactics



In addition to their interests, each actor in the intervention in Syria has also employed various strategies and tactics to achieve their goals. The Syrian government has primarily relied on military force to suppress the rebellion, using tactics such as airstrikes, artillery bombardment, and ground offensives. They have also employed propaganda and disinformation campaigns to maintain control and discredit the opposition.



Rebel groups, on the other hand, have utilized guerrilla warfare tactics, including ambushes, hit-and-run attacks, and suicide bombings. They have also received support from foreign powers, including weapons, training, and funding, to strengthen their military capabilities.



Foreign powers have also employed various strategies and tactics in their involvement in the conflict. The US and its allies have provided military and financial support to rebel groups, as well as conducting airstrikes against ISIS targets. Russia has provided direct military support to the Assad regime, including airstrikes and ground troops. Turkey has supported rebel groups and also launched military operations against Kurdish forces in northern Syria. Iran has also provided military support to the Assad regime and has deployed its own troops to the country.



Overall, the intervention in Syria has been characterized by a complex web of actors with competing interests and strategies. This has resulted in a prolonged and devastating conflict with no clear resolution in sight. 





### Section: 14.2 Analysis of Intervention:



The intervention in Syria has been a highly complex and controversial issue, with multiple actors and interests at play. In this section, we will analyze the various actors involved in the intervention and their respective interests.



#### 14.2a Actors and Interests



The key actors in the intervention in Syria include the Syrian government, rebel groups, and foreign powers. Each of these actors has their own interests and motivations for their involvement in the conflict.



The Syrian government, led by President Bashar al-Assad, has been fighting to maintain control and suppress the rebellion. Their primary interest is to maintain their power and authority over the country. The Assad regime has a long history of authoritarian rule and has been accused of human rights violations and political repression. The government's actions in response to the protests in 2011, including the use of violence, have only further solidified their desire to maintain control.



On the other hand, rebel groups in Syria have been fighting against the Assad regime in an attempt to overthrow the government and establish a new political system. These groups range from moderate opposition forces to extremist organizations such as ISIS and al-Qaeda. Their interests vary depending on their ideologies and goals, but they all share the common goal of removing Assad from power.



Foreign powers have also played a significant role in the intervention in Syria. Countries such as the United States, Russia, Turkey, and Iran have all been involved in the conflict, each with their own interests and motivations. The US and its allies have supported rebel groups in an attempt to overthrow Assad and promote democracy in the region. Russia, on the other hand, has been a staunch supporter of the Assad regime and has provided military aid to the government. Turkey and Iran have also been involved in the conflict, with Turkey supporting rebel groups and Iran supporting the Assad regime.



#### 14.2b Strategies and Tactics



In addition to their interests, each actor has also employed different strategies and tactics in the intervention. The Syrian government has primarily used military force to suppress the rebellion, including airstrikes, artillery attacks, and ground offensives. They have also employed tactics such as siege warfare and the use of chemical weapons, which have been condemned by the international community.



Rebel groups, on the other hand, have utilized a combination of guerrilla warfare and conventional tactics in their fight against the government. They have also engaged in propaganda and social media campaigns to garner support and spread their message. Some extremist groups have also resorted to terrorist tactics, such as suicide bombings and kidnappings.



Foreign powers have also employed various strategies and tactics in their involvement in the conflict. The US and its allies have provided military and financial support to rebel groups, as well as conducting airstrikes against ISIS targets. Russia has provided direct military support to the Assad regime, including air support and ground troops. Turkey has also conducted military operations in Syria, targeting both ISIS and Kurdish forces. Iran has provided financial and military support to the Assad regime, as well as deploying their own troops to fight alongside Syrian government forces.



#### 14.2c Outcomes and Impacts



The intervention in Syria has had significant outcomes and impacts on both the country and the region as a whole. The ongoing conflict has resulted in the deaths of hundreds of thousands of people and has displaced millions more. It has also caused widespread destruction and devastation, with many cities and towns reduced to rubble.



The intervention has also had political and economic impacts. The Syrian government has been weakened by the conflict, and the country's economy has been devastated. The rise of extremist groups such as ISIS has also had regional and global implications, leading to increased security concerns and the displacement of refugees.



In terms of the intervention's success, it can be argued that it has failed to achieve its intended goals. The Assad regime remains in power, and the conflict continues to rage on. The involvement of foreign powers has also complicated the situation, with competing interests and strategies often hindering progress towards a resolution.



In conclusion, the intervention in Syria has been a complex and multifaceted issue, with various actors and interests at play. The strategies and tactics employed by each actor have had significant impacts on the country and the region, but the ultimate outcome of the intervention remains uncertain. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have examined the case of intervention in Syria by great powers. We have seen how the intervention has had a significant impact on the country and its people, both positive and negative. We have also explored the various factors that led to the intervention, including political, economic, and humanitarian concerns. Through this case study, we have gained a deeper understanding of the complexities and challenges of military intervention by great powers.



The intervention in Syria serves as a cautionary tale for future interventions. It highlights the importance of careful consideration and planning before engaging in military intervention. It also emphasizes the need for a clear and achievable objective, as well as a well-defined exit strategy. The case of Syria also demonstrates the potential consequences of intervention, such as the displacement of civilians and the destabilization of the region.



As we conclude this chapter, it is essential to remember that military intervention should always be a last resort. It should only be considered when all other diplomatic and peaceful means have been exhausted. The decision to intervene should also be made with the utmost care and consideration for the well-being of the affected population.

## Chapter: Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will be examining the case study of intervention in Afghanistan by great powers. This topic is of great importance as it sheds light on the complexities and challenges of military intervention by powerful nations. Afghanistan has been a site of intervention by various great powers throughout history, making it a perfect case study to analyze the effectiveness and consequences of such actions.



We will delve into the historical context of Afghanistan and its relationship with great powers, including the Soviet Union, the United States, and NATO. We will also explore the reasons behind the interventions, such as political, economic, and strategic interests. Additionally, we will examine the methods and tactics used by these powers during their interventions and the impact it had on the country and its people.



Furthermore, we will analyze the outcomes of these interventions and their long-term effects on Afghanistan and the region. This will include the political, social, and economic consequences, as well as the role of international organizations and the local population in shaping the aftermath of these interventions.



Through this case study, we aim to provide a comprehensive understanding of great power military intervention and its implications. By examining the complexities and challenges faced in Afghanistan, we can gain valuable insights into the dynamics of intervention and its impact on both the intervening nation and the target country. 





### Section: 15.1 Background:



Afghanistan, a landlocked country in Central Asia, has been a site of intervention by great powers throughout history. Its strategic location, bordering Iran, Pakistan, and China, has made it a coveted territory for powerful nations seeking to expand their influence in the region. In this section, we will provide a brief historical context of Afghanistan and its relationship with great powers, setting the stage for our case study on intervention in the country.



#### 15.1a Historical Context



Afghanistan has a long and complex history, with various empires and dynasties ruling over the region. In the 19th and early 20th century, Afghanistan was caught in the power struggle between the British and Russian empires, known as the Great Game. The British, who controlled India, saw Afghanistan as a buffer state to protect their interests in the region, while the Russians sought to expand their influence in Central Asia.



In the 20th century, Afghanistan gained independence from British control and became a monarchy under King Amanullah Khan. However, the country faced internal conflicts and instability, leading to a series of coups and changes in leadership. In 1973, a coup led by Mohammed Daoud Khan overthrew the monarchy and established a republic.



The Soviet Union, seeking to maintain its influence in the region, supported the new government and signed a friendship treaty with Afghanistan. However, tensions between the Soviet-backed government and Islamic fundamentalist groups, known as the Mujahideen, began to escalate. In 1979, the Soviet Union intervened in Afghanistan, sending troops to support the government and suppress the Mujahideen.



The Soviet intervention sparked a decade-long war, with the Mujahideen receiving support from the United States and other Western powers. The conflict resulted in significant casualties and destruction, leading to a power vacuum and the rise of the Taliban, a fundamentalist Islamic group that took control of Afghanistan in 1996.



In response to the 9/11 attacks, the United States, with the support of NATO, launched a military intervention in Afghanistan in 2001, aiming to overthrow the Taliban and dismantle terrorist groups operating in the country, such as Al-Qaeda. The intervention resulted in the overthrow of the Taliban and the establishment of a new government, but the country continues to face ongoing conflicts and instability.



In this section, we have provided a brief overview of the historical context of Afghanistan and its relationship with great powers. In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the interventions by the Soviet Union and the United States, examining their motivations, methods, and outcomes. Through this case study, we aim to gain a comprehensive understanding of the complexities and challenges of great power military intervention in Afghanistan.





### Section: 15.1 Background:



Afghanistan, a landlocked country in Central Asia, has a long and complex history of intervention by great powers. Its strategic location, bordering Iran, Pakistan, and China, has made it a coveted territory for powerful nations seeking to expand their influence in the region. In this section, we will provide a brief historical context of Afghanistan and its relationship with great powers, setting the stage for our case study on intervention in the country.



#### 15.1a Historical Context



Afghanistan has been a site of conflict and conquest for centuries. In the 19th and early 20th century, the country was caught in the power struggle between the British and Russian empires, known as the Great Game. The British, who controlled India, saw Afghanistan as a buffer state to protect their interests in the region, while the Russians sought to expand their influence in Central Asia.



In the 20th century, Afghanistan gained independence from British control and became a monarchy under King Amanullah Khan. However, the country faced internal conflicts and instability, leading to a series of coups and changes in leadership. In 1973, a coup led by Mohammed Daoud Khan overthrew the monarchy and established a republic.



The Soviet Union, seeking to maintain its influence in the region, supported the new government and signed a friendship treaty with Afghanistan. However, tensions between the Soviet-backed government and Islamic fundamentalist groups, known as the Mujahideen, began to escalate. In 1979, the Soviet Union intervened in Afghanistan, sending troops to support the government and suppress the Mujahideen.



The Soviet intervention sparked a decade-long war, with the Mujahideen receiving support from the United States and other Western powers. The conflict resulted in significant casualties and destruction, leading to a power vacuum and the rise of the Taliban, a fundamentalist Islamic group that took control of the country in 1996.



#### 15.1b The Afghan Conflict



The conflict in Afghanistan has been ongoing for decades, with various groups vying for control of the country. In recent years, the Taliban has regained power and established an Islamic emirate, leading to a resurgence of violence and instability.



In 2021, the Taliban launched a major offensive, capturing several key cities, including Kunduz. The fall of Kunduz led to the sacking of Afghan army chief, Gen. Wali Ahmadzai, and further destabilized the country.



As the Taliban took over Kabul, a Coordination Council was formed to transfer power to the Taliban, consisting of former mujahideen and Hezb-e Islami commander Gulbuddin Hekmatyar, former President Hamid Karzai, and political leader Abdullah Abdullah. However, it was reported that Karzai and Abdullah were effectively placed under house arrest by the Taliban.



In response to the Taliban's takeover, anti-Taliban uprisings have emerged, including the National Resistance Front of Afghanistan led by Amrullah Saleh and Ahmad Massoud. These uprisings have resulted in small-scale victories against the Taliban, but the country remains in a state of turmoil.



The ongoing conflict in Afghanistan highlights the complex dynamics of great power intervention and its impact on the country's stability and security. In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the case study of intervention in Afghanistan and analyze the various factors at play.





### Section: 15.1 Background:



Afghanistan, a landlocked country in Central Asia, has a long and complex history of intervention by great powers. Its strategic location, bordering Iran, Pakistan, and China, has made it a coveted territory for powerful nations seeking to expand their influence in the region. In this section, we will provide a brief historical context of Afghanistan and its relationship with great powers, setting the stage for our case study on intervention in the country.



#### 15.1a Historical Context



Afghanistan has been a site of conflict and conquest for centuries. In the 19th and early 20th century, the country was caught in the power struggle between the British and Russian empires, known as the Great Game. The British, who controlled India, saw Afghanistan as a buffer state to protect their interests in the region, while the Russians sought to expand their influence in Central Asia.



In the 20th century, Afghanistan gained independence from British control and became a monarchy under King Amanullah Khan. However, the country faced internal conflicts and instability, leading to a series of coups and changes in leadership. In 1973, a coup led by Mohammed Daoud Khan overthrew the monarchy and established a republic.



The Soviet Union, seeking to maintain its influence in the region, supported the new government and signed a friendship treaty with Afghanistan. However, tensions between the Soviet-backed government and Islamic fundamentalist groups, known as the Mujahideen, began to escalate. In 1979, the Soviet Union intervened in Afghanistan, sending troops to support the government and suppress the Mujahideen.



The Soviet intervention sparked a decade-long war, with the Mujahideen receiving support from the United States and other Western powers. The conflict resulted in significant casualties and destruction, leading to a power vacuum and the rise of the Taliban, a fundamentalist Islamic group that took control of the country in 1996.



#### 15.1b International Response



The Soviet intervention in Afghanistan was met with strong international condemnation and opposition. The United States, along with other Western powers, saw the Soviet intervention as a threat to their interests in the region and provided support to the Mujahideen in the form of weapons and training. This support was part of the larger Cold War rivalry between the United States and the Soviet Union.



In addition to the support for the Mujahideen, the international community also imposed economic sanctions on the Soviet Union in response to their intervention. These sanctions, along with the ongoing war, put a strain on the Soviet economy and contributed to the eventual collapse of the Soviet Union in 1991.



#### 15.1c Lessons Learned



The intervention in Afghanistan serves as a case study for the importance of international communication and coordination in responding to conflicts and crises. The lack of coordination and cooperation between the Soviet Union and the United States, as well as other international actors, prolonged the conflict and resulted in significant human and economic costs.



Furthermore, the intervention highlighted the need for strong international coalitions and plans of action in responding to crises. The lack of a coordinated response from the international community allowed the conflict to escalate and resulted in unintended consequences, such as the rise of the Taliban.



In addition, the intervention in Afghanistan also demonstrated the importance of understanding the local context and dynamics of a conflict before intervening. The Soviet Union's lack of understanding of the cultural and political landscape in Afghanistan, as well as their support for a government that was not widely accepted by the Afghan people, ultimately led to their failure in the country.



Overall, the intervention in Afghanistan serves as a cautionary tale for future interventions and highlights the need for effective communication, coordination, and understanding of local contexts in responding to conflicts and crises. 





### Section: 15.2 Analysis of Intervention:



In this section, we will analyze the intervention in Afghanistan by examining the various actors and their interests in the conflict. The intervention in Afghanistan has been a complex and ongoing issue, involving multiple great powers and local actors. By understanding the motivations and actions of these actors, we can gain insight into the dynamics of intervention and its impact on the country.



#### 15.2a Actors and Interests



The intervention in Afghanistan has involved a range of actors, including great powers such as the Soviet Union, the United States, and neighboring countries like Pakistan and Iran. Each of these actors had their own interests and motivations for intervening in the conflict.



The Soviet Union, seeking to maintain its influence in the region, intervened in Afghanistan in 1979 to support the communist government and suppress the Mujahideen. This intervention was driven by their desire to prevent the spread of Islamic fundamentalism and protect their strategic interests in the region.



On the other hand, the United States saw the Soviet intervention as an opportunity to weaken their Cold War rival. They provided support to the Mujahideen, seeing them as a potential ally in their fight against communism. This support included weapons, training, and funding, which ultimately contributed to the Soviet Union's defeat and withdrawal from Afghanistan in 1989.



Neighboring countries like Pakistan and Iran also played a significant role in the intervention in Afghanistan. Pakistan, with its own interests in the region, provided support to the Mujahideen and served as a base for their operations. Iran, on the other hand, had a more complicated relationship with Afghanistan, with both cooperation and conflict occurring between the two countries.



Local actors, such as the Mujahideen and the Taliban, also had their own interests and motivations for participating in the conflict. The Mujahideen, a group of Islamic fundamentalists, sought to overthrow the communist government and establish an Islamic state. The Taliban, a fundamentalist group that emerged in the aftermath of the Soviet withdrawal, aimed to establish a strict Islamic government in Afghanistan.



The interests of these various actors often clashed, leading to a complex and prolonged conflict in Afghanistan. The intervention by great powers and their support for different local actors further complicated the situation and had long-lasting effects on the country.



In conclusion, the intervention in Afghanistan involved a range of actors with diverse interests and motivations. By understanding these actors and their actions, we can gain a better understanding of the dynamics of intervention and its impact on the country. 





### Section: 15.2 Analysis of Intervention:



In this section, we will analyze the intervention in Afghanistan by examining the various actors and their interests in the conflict. The intervention in Afghanistan has been a complex and ongoing issue, involving multiple great powers and local actors. By understanding the motivations and actions of these actors, we can gain insight into the dynamics of intervention and its impact on the country.



#### 15.2a Actors and Interests



The intervention in Afghanistan has involved a range of actors, including great powers such as the Soviet Union, the United States, and neighboring countries like Pakistan and Iran. Each of these actors had their own interests and motivations for intervening in the conflict.



The Soviet Union, seeking to maintain its influence in the region, intervened in Afghanistan in 1979 to support the communist government and suppress the Mujahideen. This intervention was driven by their desire to prevent the spread of Islamic fundamentalism and protect their strategic interests in the region. However, their intervention ultimately proved to be a costly and unsuccessful endeavor, leading to their withdrawal in 1989.



On the other hand, the United States saw the Soviet intervention as an opportunity to weaken their Cold War rival. They provided support to the Mujahideen, seeing them as a potential ally in their fight against communism. This support included weapons, training, and funding, which ultimately contributed to the Soviet Union's defeat and withdrawal from Afghanistan in 1989. However, this support also had unintended consequences, as it helped to fuel the rise of extremist groups like the Taliban.



Neighboring countries like Pakistan and Iran also played a significant role in the intervention in Afghanistan. Pakistan, with its own interests in the region, provided support to the Mujahideen and served as a base for their operations. This support was crucial in the Mujahideen's fight against the Soviet Union. However, it also led to the rise of extremist groups within Pakistan, causing instability in the region.



Iran, on the other hand, had a more complicated relationship with Afghanistan, with both cooperation and conflict occurring between the two countries. Iran initially supported the Mujahideen in their fight against the Soviet Union, but later became involved in the conflict due to their own interests in the region. This involvement further complicated the already complex dynamics of the intervention in Afghanistan.



Local actors, such as the Mujahideen and the Taliban, also had their own interests and motivations for participating in the conflict. The Mujahideen, a group of Islamic fighters, were initially united in their fight against the Soviet Union. However, after the Soviet Union's withdrawal, they turned against each other, leading to a civil war that further destabilized the country.



The Taliban, a radical Islamic group, emerged in the 1990s and gained control of Afghanistan in 1996. They were initially supported by Pakistan and had the goal of establishing a strict Islamic state in Afghanistan. Their rise to power was fueled by the chaos and instability caused by the intervention of great powers and local actors.



In conclusion, the intervention in Afghanistan involved a complex web of actors with their own interests and motivations. This intervention had far-reaching consequences, leading to the rise of extremist groups and causing instability in the region. By understanding the dynamics of this intervention, we can gain insight into the complexities of military intervention and its impact on a country and its people.





### Section: 15.2 Analysis of Intervention:



In this section, we will analyze the intervention in Afghanistan by examining the various actors and their interests in the conflict. The intervention in Afghanistan has been a complex and ongoing issue, involving multiple great powers and local actors. By understanding the motivations and actions of these actors, we can gain insight into the dynamics of intervention and its impact on the country.



#### 15.2a Actors and Interests



The intervention in Afghanistan has involved a range of actors, including great powers such as the Soviet Union, the United States, and neighboring countries like Pakistan and Iran. Each of these actors had their own interests and motivations for intervening in the conflict.



The Soviet Union, seeking to maintain its influence in the region, intervened in Afghanistan in 1979 to support the communist government and suppress the Mujahideen. This intervention was driven by their desire to prevent the spread of Islamic fundamentalism and protect their strategic interests in the region. However, their intervention ultimately proved to be a costly and unsuccessful endeavor, leading to their withdrawal in 1989.



On the other hand, the United States saw the Soviet intervention as an opportunity to weaken their Cold War rival. They provided support to the Mujahideen, seeing them as a potential ally in their fight against communism. This support included weapons, training, and funding, which ultimately contributed to the Soviet Union's defeat and withdrawal from Afghanistan in 1989. However, this support also had unintended consequences, as it helped to fuel the rise of extremist groups like the Taliban.



Neighboring countries like Pakistan and Iran also played a significant role in the intervention in Afghanistan. Pakistan, with its own interests in the region, provided support to the Mujahideen and served as a base for their operations. This support was crucial in the Mujahideen's fight against the Soviet Union, but it also had long-lasting effects on the country. The influx of weapons and training from Pakistan contributed to the militarization of Afghan society and the rise of warlords, leading to ongoing conflicts and instability.



Iran, on the other hand, had a more complex relationship with Afghanistan. While initially supporting the Mujahideen, they later shifted their support to the Northern Alliance, a coalition of anti-Taliban groups. This support was driven by their desire to counter the influence of the Taliban, who were predominantly Sunni Muslims, while Iran is a Shia Muslim country. However, their involvement also contributed to the fragmentation of the country and ongoing conflicts.



#### 15.2b Strategies and Tactics



In addition to understanding the motivations and interests of the various actors involved in the intervention, it is also important to analyze the strategies and tactics used by these actors. The Soviet Union, for example, employed a conventional military approach, using their superior firepower and resources to try and defeat the Mujahideen. However, this approach proved to be ineffective against the guerrilla tactics used by the Mujahideen.



The United States, on the other hand, used a combination of covert operations and support for the Mujahideen to achieve their goals. This included providing weapons and training, as well as using their intelligence agencies to gather information and disrupt Soviet operations. This approach proved to be successful in achieving their goal of defeating the Soviet Union, but it also had unintended consequences, as mentioned earlier.



Neighboring countries like Pakistan and Iran also used different strategies and tactics in their involvement in the intervention. Pakistan, for example, used its geographical proximity to Afghanistan to provide support and safe havens for the Mujahideen. They also used their influence to shape the direction of the conflict, often supporting certain factions over others. Iran, on the other hand, used their support for the Northern Alliance to gain influence and counter the Taliban's influence in the region.



#### 15.2c Outcomes and Impacts



The intervention in Afghanistan has had far-reaching consequences for the country and the region. The Soviet Union's defeat and subsequent withdrawal led to a power vacuum, which was filled by various factions and warlords. This, in turn, led to ongoing conflicts and instability in the country, as different groups vied for power and control.



The United States' support for the Mujahideen also had unintended consequences, as it helped to fuel the rise of extremist groups like the Taliban. The Taliban's rise to power and their subsequent rule had a devastating impact on the country, particularly for women and minority groups. The ongoing conflict and instability in Afghanistan have also had regional and global impacts, including the spread of terrorism and the displacement of millions of people.



In conclusion, the intervention in Afghanistan by various great powers and neighboring countries has had a significant impact on the country and the region. By analyzing the motivations, strategies, and outcomes of these interventions, we can gain a better understanding of the complexities of military intervention and its long-lasting effects. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have examined the case of intervention in Afghanistan by great powers. We have seen that this intervention has been a complex and controversial issue, with various factors at play. From the initial Soviet intervention in the 1980s to the ongoing US-led intervention, we have seen how different motivations, strategies, and outcomes have shaped the course of events in Afghanistan.



One of the key takeaways from this case study is the importance of understanding the local context and dynamics when considering military intervention. The Soviet Union's failure in Afghanistan can be attributed in part to their lack of understanding of the country's history, culture, and tribal dynamics. Similarly, the US-led intervention has faced challenges due to its limited understanding of the complexities of Afghan society.



Furthermore, this case study highlights the potential consequences of intervention, both intended and unintended. While the Soviet Union's intervention was aimed at maintaining a communist government in Afghanistan, it ultimately led to the rise of the Taliban and the spread of radical Islamist ideology. Similarly, the US-led intervention, while initially successful in overthrowing the Taliban, has resulted in a prolonged and costly conflict with no clear end in sight.



Overall, the case of intervention in Afghanistan serves as a cautionary tale for great powers considering military intervention in other countries. It emphasizes the need for a thorough understanding of the local context, clear objectives, and a well-defined exit strategy to avoid unintended consequences and prolonged conflicts.
