#### Exercise 1

Research and analyze a different case study of great power military intervention and compare it to the intervention in Iraq. What were the similarities and differences in terms of objectives, strategies, and outcomes?



#### Exercise 2

Discuss the ethical considerations of great power military intervention. Is it ever justified? What are the potential consequences and responsibilities of intervening in another country's affairs?



#### Exercise 3

## Chapter: Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



### Introduction



Military intervention by great powers has been a topic of great interest and debate in international relations. It involves the use of military force by a powerful nation or group of nations to intervene in the affairs of another country. This can take various forms, such as direct military intervention, indirect support for rebel groups, or humanitarian intervention. In this chapter, we will focus on a specific case study of great power military intervention in Somalia.



Somalia, a country located in the Horn of Africa, has been plagued by political instability, civil war, and humanitarian crises for decades. In 1992, the United Nations authorized a multinational intervention force, led by the United States, to provide humanitarian aid and restore order in the country. This intervention, known as Operation Restore Hope, marked the first time since the end of the Cold War that the United States had led a major military operation without the support of its allies.



The intervention in Somalia was met with both praise and criticism. On one hand, it successfully provided much-needed aid to the Somali people and helped to restore some level of stability in the country. On the other hand, it also highlighted the challenges and limitations of great power military intervention, as well as the potential consequences of such actions.



In this chapter, we will examine the various factors that led to the intervention in Somalia, the objectives and strategies of the intervening powers, and the outcomes and consequences of the intervention. We will also discuss the lessons learned from this case study and their implications for future great power military interventions. By studying this specific example, we hope to gain a better understanding of the complexities and dynamics of great power military intervention and its impact on both the intervening powers and the target country.





# Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



## Chapter 19: Case Study - Intervention in Somalia



### Section 19.1 Background



#### 19.1a Historical Context



The late modern period, spanning from 1815 to 1914, was characterized by European dominance and the rise of great powers. During this time, the concept of imperialism and the pursuit of empires became prevalent among European nations. This led to the decline and eventual dissolution of once powerful empires such as Spain, Ottoman Turkey, the Mughal Empire, and the Kingdom of Portugal.



Spain, which was once unrivaled in Europe, had been in decline for some time before being weakened by Napoleon Bonaparte's invasion. This created an opportunity for Spain's colonies in South America to rebel and gain their independence.



The Ottoman Empire, which had once been a vast and powerful empire, was plagued by revolutions and eventually only held a small region surrounding its capital, Istanbul.



The Mughal Empire, which was descended from the Mongol Khanate, was eventually overtaken by the Maratha Confederacy. However, the British, with their superior military and economic power, were able to gain control over much of India and its surrounding regions.



Similarly, the Kingdom of Portugal's vast territory of Brazil eventually gained its independence and formed the Empire of Brazil. Meanwhile, Britain emerged as the most powerful country in the world, controlling a quarter of the world's population and a third of its surface. The British Empire also had the most powerful navy at the time, solidifying their dominance.



The late modern period also saw the rise of Germany as a great international power, thanks to advancements in technology such as electricity, steel, and petroleum. This led to Germany's pursuit of creating its own empire.



The decolonization of the Americas also occurred during this time, through various revolutions and wars. This period of European dominance and imperialism set the stage for future great power interventions, including the intervention in Somalia.



## Chapter 19: Case Study - Intervention in Somalia



### Section 19.1 Background

In this chapter, we will examine the various factors that led to the intervention in Somalia, the objectives and strategies of the intervening powers, and the outcomes and consequences of the intervention. We will also discuss the lessons learned from this case study and their implications for future great power military interventions. By studying this specific example, we hope to gain a better understanding of the dynamics and impact of great power military intervention.





# Great Power Military Intervention: A Comprehensive Study



## Chapter 19: Case Study - Intervention in Somalia



### Section 19.1 Background



#### 19.1a Historical Context



The late modern period, spanning from 1815 to 1914, was characterized by European dominance and the rise of great powers. During this time, the concept of imperialism and the pursuit of empires became prevalent among European nations. This led to the decline and eventual dissolution of once powerful empires such as Spain, Ottoman Turkey, the Mughal Empire, and the Kingdom of Portugal.



Spain, which was once unrivaled in Europe, had been in decline for some time before being weakened by Napoleon Bonaparte's invasion. This created an opportunity for Spain's colonies in South America to rebel and gain their independence.



The Ottoman Empire, which had once been a vast and powerful empire, was plagued by revolutions and eventually only held a small region surrounding its capital, Istanbul.



The Mughal Empire, which was descended from the Mongol Khanate, was eventually overtaken by the Maratha Confederacy. However, the British, with their superior military and economic power, were able to gain control over much of India and its surrounding regions.



Similarly, the Kingdom of Portugal's vast territory of Brazil eventually gained its independence and formed the Empire of Brazil. Meanwhile, Britain emerged as the most powerful country in the world, controlling a quarter of the world's population and a third of its surface. The British Empire also had the most powerful navy at the time, solidifying their dominance.



The late modern period also saw the rise of Germany as a great international power, thanks to advancements in technology such as electricity, steel, and petroleum. This led to Germany's pursuit of creating its own empire.



The decolonization of the Americas also occurred during this time, through various revolutions and wars. This period of European dominance and imperialism set the stage for the emergence of new global powers and the competition for resources and territories.



### Subsection 19.1b The Somali Conflict



The Somali conflict is a complex and ongoing conflict that has been characterized by political instability, violence, and humanitarian crises. The roots of the conflict can be traced back to the late 19th century when European powers, particularly Britain, France, and Italy, began colonizing the region.



In the late 19th and early 20th centuries, Somalia was divided into several colonies, with Britain controlling the northern region, Italy controlling the southern region, and France controlling the Djibouti region. This division created artificial borders that did not align with the ethnic and cultural divisions of the Somali people, leading to tensions and conflicts.



After gaining independence in 1960, Somalia experienced a period of political instability and military coups. In the 1980s, the country was ruled by a military dictatorship under Siad Barre, which led to widespread human rights abuses and economic decline.



In 1991, the Somali Civil War broke out, leading to the collapse of the central government and the emergence of various armed groups and warlords. The conflict has been fueled by ethnic and clan divisions, competition for resources, and external interventions.



One of the major interventions in the Somali conflict was the United States' military intervention in 1992, which aimed to provide humanitarian aid and restore stability. However, the intervention was met with resistance and resulted in the deaths of thousands of Somalis and the withdrawal of US forces in 1994.



In the early 2000s, the conflict continued to escalate, with various armed groups vying for control of the country. In 2006, the Islamic Courts Union (ICU) gained control of Mogadishu, the capital of Somalia, and established a strict form of Islamic law. This led to the intervention of neighboring Ethiopia, which supported the Transitional Federal Government (TFG) in ousting the ICU.



The intervention by Ethiopia and its support for the TFG sparked a new wave of violence and insurgency, with the emergence of the militant group Al-Shabaab. The conflict has also spilled over into neighboring countries, particularly Kenya and Ethiopia, leading to cross-border clashes and displacement of people.



In recent years, the conflict has been further complicated by the involvement of other external actors, such as the United Nations, African Union, and various international organizations. Despite efforts to find a political solution and restore stability, the Somali conflict continues to have devastating effects on the country and its people.





### Section: 19.1 Background:



The intervention in Somalia by great powers is a complex and controversial topic that requires a thorough understanding of the historical context and international response. In this section, we will explore the background of Somalia and its relationship with the international community, leading up to the intervention.



#### 19.1a Historical Context:



Somalia, located in the Horn of Africa, has a long history of conflict and instability. The country was colonized by European powers in the late 19th century, with Britain controlling the northern region and Italy controlling the southern region. After gaining independence in 1960, Somalia experienced a series of coups and civil wars, leading to the collapse of its government in 1991.



The lack of a stable government and ongoing conflicts have resulted in a humanitarian crisis in Somalia, with millions of people facing food insecurity and displacement. The country has also become a safe haven for terrorist groups, such as Al-Shabaab, which has carried out attacks both within Somalia and in neighboring countries.



The international community has been involved in Somalia since the early 1990s, providing humanitarian aid and attempting to facilitate peace and stability. However, these efforts have been largely unsuccessful due to the complex political and social dynamics within Somalia.



### Subsection: 19.1c International Response:



The international response to the crisis in Somalia has been multifaceted and constantly evolving. In 1992, the United Nations Security Council authorized a peacekeeping mission, known as UNOSOM I, to provide humanitarian aid and facilitate political reconciliation. However, the mission was met with resistance and violence from local warlords, leading to the withdrawal of UN forces in 1995.



In 2006, the Islamic Courts Union (ICU) gained control of much of southern Somalia, including the capital city of Mogadishu. This sparked concerns among neighboring countries and the international community, as the ICU had ties to terrorist organizations. In response, Ethiopia, with the support of the United States, launched a military intervention to oust the ICU and install a transitional government.



The intervention was met with mixed reactions, with some viewing it as necessary to combat terrorism and others criticizing it as a violation of Somalia's sovereignty. The intervention also had unintended consequences, such as fueling anti-Ethiopian sentiment and leading to an increase in violence and instability.



In 2007, the United Nations authorized a new peacekeeping mission, known as AMISOM, to support the transitional government and stabilize the country. The mission has been ongoing, with various countries contributing troops and resources.



In recent years, there have been efforts to promote political reconciliation and establish a stable government in Somalia. In 2012, a new constitution was adopted, and a new federal government was formed. However, the country continues to face challenges, including ongoing violence and humanitarian crises.



The international community remains involved in Somalia, providing aid and support to promote peace and stability. However, the effectiveness of these efforts is still a subject of debate, and the situation in Somalia remains complex and volatile.





### Section: 19.2 Analysis of Intervention:



In this section, we will analyze the intervention in Somalia by great powers, focusing on the actors involved and their interests.



#### 19.2a Actors and Interests:



The intervention in Somalia involved multiple actors, including the United Nations, the United States, and neighboring countries such as Ethiopia and Kenya. Each of these actors had their own interests and motivations for getting involved in the conflict.



The United Nations, as an international organization, had a primary interest in promoting peace and stability in Somalia and providing humanitarian aid to the millions of people affected by the ongoing conflict. However, the UN also faced pressure from member states to take action against terrorist groups operating in Somalia, such as Al-Shabaab.



The United States, as a global superpower, also had a vested interest in the situation in Somalia. The US saw the presence of terrorist groups in Somalia as a threat to its national security and therefore had a strong motivation to intervene and eliminate these groups.



Neighboring countries, such as Ethiopia and Kenya, also had their own interests in the intervention. These countries were concerned about the spillover effects of the conflict in Somalia, such as refugee flows and the spread of terrorism. As a result, they saw the intervention as a way to protect their own national interests.



Overall, the main interests of the actors involved in the intervention in Somalia were to promote peace and stability, eliminate terrorist threats, and protect their own national interests. However, these interests often conflicted with each other, leading to complex dynamics and challenges in achieving a successful intervention.





### Section: 19.2 Analysis of Intervention:



In this section, we will analyze the intervention in Somalia by great powers, focusing on the strategies and tactics employed by the various actors involved.



#### 19.2b Strategies and Tactics:



The intervention in Somalia was a complex and multifaceted operation, involving a variety of strategies and tactics from the different actors involved. In this subsection, we will examine some of the key strategies and tactics used by the United Nations, the United States, and neighboring countries in their intervention efforts.



The United Nations, as an international organization, utilized a combination of diplomatic, humanitarian, and military strategies in their intervention in Somalia. Diplomatically, the UN worked to broker peace agreements between warring factions and facilitate negotiations for a political solution to the conflict. Humanitarian aid was also a key aspect of the UN's intervention, as they provided food, shelter, and medical assistance to the millions of people affected by the conflict. In terms of military tactics, the UN deployed peacekeeping forces to maintain stability and protect civilians in the region.



The United States, as a global superpower, primarily employed military strategies in their intervention in Somalia. This included airstrikes and ground operations targeting terrorist groups, as well as training and equipping local forces to combat these groups. The US also utilized diplomatic and economic pressure to encourage other countries to join the intervention efforts.



Neighboring countries, such as Ethiopia and Kenya, also played a significant role in the intervention in Somalia. They employed a combination of military and diplomatic strategies, including deploying their own troops to support the UN peacekeeping mission and engaging in negotiations with various factions to promote peace and stability in the region.



Overall, the strategies and tactics used in the intervention in Somalia were aimed at achieving the main goals of promoting peace and stability, eliminating terrorist threats, and protecting national interests. However, the success of these strategies and tactics was often hindered by the complex and volatile nature of the conflict, as well as conflicting interests among the various actors involved. 





### Section: 19.2 Analysis of Intervention:



In this section, we will analyze the intervention in Somalia by great powers, focusing on the strategies and tactics employed by the various actors involved.



#### 19.2b Strategies and Tactics:



The intervention in Somalia was a complex and multifaceted operation, involving a variety of strategies and tactics from the different actors involved. In this subsection, we will examine some of the key strategies and tactics used by the United Nations, the United States, and neighboring countries in their intervention efforts.



The United Nations, as an international organization, utilized a combination of diplomatic, humanitarian, and military strategies in their intervention in Somalia. Diplomatically, the UN worked to broker peace agreements between warring factions and facilitate negotiations for a political solution to the conflict. This approach was based on the belief that a peaceful resolution would be the most effective and sustainable way to address the conflict in Somalia. However, the UN also recognized the need for humanitarian aid to address the immediate needs of the population affected by the conflict. This included providing food, shelter, and medical assistance to millions of people in Somalia.



In terms of military strategies, the UN deployed peacekeeping forces to maintain stability and protect civilians in the region. This was a crucial aspect of the intervention, as it aimed to create a safe and secure environment for the delivery of humanitarian aid and the implementation of diplomatic efforts. The UN peacekeeping forces were also responsible for disarming and demobilizing armed groups in Somalia, with the goal of reducing violence and promoting peace.



The United States, as a global superpower, primarily employed military strategies in their intervention in Somalia. This included airstrikes and ground operations targeting terrorist groups, as well as training and equipping local forces to combat these groups. The US also utilized diplomatic and economic pressure to encourage other countries to join the intervention efforts. This approach was driven by the US's belief that military force was necessary to combat the threat of terrorism in Somalia and protect its national security interests.



Neighboring countries, such as Ethiopia and Kenya, also played a significant role in the intervention in Somalia. They employed a combination of military and diplomatic strategies, including deploying their own troops to support the UN peacekeeping mission and engaging in negotiations with various factions to promote peace and stability in the region. These countries were directly affected by the conflict in Somalia and had a vested interest in resolving it. Their involvement was crucial in providing support and resources to the UN and the US in their intervention efforts.



Overall, the strategies and tactics used in the intervention in Somalia were a reflection of the complex nature of the conflict and the diverse interests of the actors involved. While the UN focused on diplomatic and humanitarian efforts, the US prioritized military action, and neighboring countries played a role in both. This combination of strategies and tactics ultimately led to varying outcomes and impacts, which will be further explored in the next subsection.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have examined the case study of intervention in Somalia by great powers. We have seen how the intervention was motivated by various factors such as humanitarian concerns, regional stability, and national interests. We have also discussed the different approaches taken by the intervening powers and the challenges they faced in achieving their objectives. From this case study, we can draw several conclusions about the effectiveness and consequences of great power military intervention.



Firstly, we have seen that great power intervention can have both positive and negative outcomes. In the case of Somalia, the intervention helped to alleviate the humanitarian crisis and restore some level of stability. However, it also resulted in unintended consequences such as the rise of warlords and the spread of terrorism. This highlights the need for careful consideration and planning before engaging in military intervention.



Secondly, the success of great power intervention depends on the cooperation and coordination among the intervening powers. In the case of Somalia, the lack of coordination between the US and UN forces led to confusion and hindered the effectiveness of the intervention. This emphasizes the importance of clear communication and a unified strategy among the intervening powers.



Lastly, great power intervention should not be seen as a long-term solution to conflicts. While it may provide temporary relief and stability, it is ultimately up to the local actors to address the root causes of the conflict and work towards a sustainable solution. This highlights the need for a comprehensive approach that includes diplomatic, economic, and social measures in addition to military intervention.



In conclusion, the case study of intervention in Somalia serves as a valuable lesson for future great power interventions. It highlights the complexities and challenges involved in such interventions and emphasizes the need for careful consideration and coordination among the intervening powers. It also reminds us that military intervention should not be seen as a panacea for conflicts, but rather as a temporary measure to address immediate crises.

### Section: 20.1 Recap and Reflection:

One of the key takeaways from our study is that great power military intervention is often driven by a combination of political, economic, and strategic interests. While some interventions may be motivated by a desire to promote democracy or protect human rights, others may be driven by a desire to gain access to resources or expand influence in a particular region. It is important to recognize these underlying motivations in order to fully understand the dynamics of military intervention.



Another important takeaway is the significant impact that military intervention can have on both the target country and the global political landscape. While interventions may achieve their intended objectives, they can also have unintended consequences and lead to long-term instability and conflict. It is crucial for great powers to carefully consider the potential consequences of their actions before intervening in the affairs of other countries.



Furthermore, our study has also highlighted the ethical considerations surrounding military intervention. While some argue that it is necessary to intervene in order to protect innocent civilians or promote democracy, others argue that it is a violation of a country's sovereignty and can lead to further harm. It is important for great powers to carefully consider the ethical implications of their actions and to seek international support and legitimacy before intervening.



### Subsection: 20.1a Key Takeaways

In addition to reviewing the material, we will also discuss the impact of interventions on both the intervening countries and the target countries. This includes the political, economic, and social consequences of interventions, as well as the ethical considerations and controversies surrounding them.



During the review session, we encourage you to ask questions and engage in critical thinking about the complexities of great power military intervention. This will not only help you prepare for the exam, but also deepen your understanding of this important topic.



We hope that this review session will help you feel confident and prepared for the final exam. Remember to manage your time effectively and avoid cramming. We wish you the best of luck on your exam and look forward to seeing your thoughtful responses.
